      <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fermi_Observations_of_Dwarf_Galaxies_Provide_New_Insights_on_Dark_Matter.ogv" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">\n          Fermi-LAT\n         </a>\n         observations of dwarf galaxies provide new insights on dark matter.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Baryonic_matter">\n        Baryonic matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Baryonic matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Not to be confused with\n       <a href="/wiki/Missing_baryon_problem" title="Missing baryon problem">\n        Missing baryon problem\n       </a>\n       .\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">\n        Baryons\n       </a>\n       (\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        protons\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">\n        neutrons\n       </a>\n       ) make up ordinary stars and planets. However, baryonic matter also encompasses less common\n       <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">\n        black holes\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">\n        neutron stars\n       </a>\n       , faint old\n       <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">\n        white dwarfs\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">\n        brown dwarfs\n       </a>\n       , collectively known as\n       <a href="/wiki/Massive_compact_halo_object" title="Massive compact halo object">\n        massive compact halo objects\n       </a>\n       (MACHOs).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015286_76-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015286-76">\n         [74]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This ordinary, but hard to see, matter could explain dark matter.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       However multiple lines of evidence suggest the majority of dark matter is not made of baryons:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        Sufficient diffuse, baryonic gas or dust would be visible when backlit by stars.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        The theory of\n        <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">\n         Big Bang nucleosynthesis\n        </a>\n        predicts the observed\n        <a href="/wiki/Abundance_of_the_chemical_elements" title="Abundance of the chemical elements">\n         abundance of the chemical elements\n        </a>\n        . If there are more baryons, then there should also be more helium, lithium and heavier elements synthesized during the Big Bang.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">\n         <a href="#cite_note-77">\n          [75]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">\n         <a href="#cite_note-78">\n          [76]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        Agreement with observed abundances requires that baryonic matter makes up between 4\xe2\x80\x935% of the universe\'s\n        <a href="/wiki/Friedmann_equations#Density_parameter" title="Friedmann equations">\n         critical density\n        </a>\n        . In contrast,\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_universe" title="Large-scale structure of the universe">\n         large-scale structure\n        </a>\n        and other observations indicate that the total matter density is about 30% of the critical density.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-planckesa2015_71-1">\n         <a href="#cite_note-planckesa2015-71">\n          [69]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Astronomical searches for\n        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_microlensing" title="Gravitational microlensing">\n         gravitational microlensing\n        </a>\n        in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">\n         Milky Way\n        </a>\n        found that at most a small fraction of the dark matter may be in dark, compact, conventional objects (MACHOs, etc.); the excluded range of object masses is from half the Earth\'s mass up to 30 solar masses, which covers nearly all the plausible candidates.\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">\n         <a href="#cite_note-79">\n          [77]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">\n         <a href="#cite_note-80">\n          [78]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">\n         <a href="#cite_note-81">\n          [79]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">\n         <a href="#cite_note-82">\n          [80]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">\n         <a href="#cite_note-83">\n          [81]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">\n         <a href="#cite_note-84">\n          [82]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Detailed analysis of the small irregularities (anisotropies) in the\n        <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_microwave_background" title="Cosmic microwave background">\n         cosmic microwave background\n        </a>\n        .\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">\n         <a href="#cite_note-85">\n          [83]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        Observations by\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">\n         WMAP\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Planck_(spacecraft)" title="Planck (spacecraft)">\n         Planck\n        </a>\n        indicate that around five-sixths of the total matter is in a form that interacts significantly with ordinary matter or\n        <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">\n         photons\n        </a>\n        only through gravitational effects.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-baryonic_matter">\n        Non-baryonic matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Non-baryonic matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Candidates for nonbaryonic dark matter are hypothetical particles such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">\n        axions\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">\n        sterile neutrinos\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">\n        WIMPs\n       </a>\n       (e.g.\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersymmetric" title="Supersymmetric">\n        supersymmetric\n       </a>\n       particles). The three neutrino types already observed are indeed abundant, and \xe2\x80\x9cdark\xe2\x80\x9d, and matter, but because their individual masses \xe2\x80\x93 however uncertain they may be \xe2\x80\x93 are almost certainly tiny, they can only supply a small fraction of dark matter, due to limits derived from\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Large-scale_structure_of_the_universe" title="Large-scale structure of the universe">\n        large-scale structure\n       </a>\n       and high-\n       <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">\n        redshift\n       </a>\n       galaxies.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_86-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-86">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Unlike baryonic matter, nonbaryonic matter did not contribute to the formation of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">\n        elements\n       </a>\n       in the early universe ("\n       <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">\n        Big Bang nucleosynthesis\n       </a>\n       ")\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and so its presence is revealed only via its gravitational effects. In addition, if the particles of which it is composed are supersymmetric, they can undergo\n       <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">\n        annihilation\n       </a>\n       interactions with themselves, possibly resulting in observable by-products such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">\n        gamma rays\n       </a>\n       and neutrinos ("indirect detection").\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_86-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-86">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span id="Classification_of_dark_matter:_cold,_warm_or_hot">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_dark_matter:_cold.2C_warm_or_hot">\n        Classification of dark matter: cold, warm or hot\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Classification of dark matter: cold, warm or hot">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       Dark matter can be divided into\n       <i>\n        cold\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        warm\n       </i>\n       and\n       <i>\n        hot\n       </i>\n       categories.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">\n        <a href="#cite_note-87">\n         [85]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       These categories refer to velocity rather than an actual temperature, indicating how far corresponding objects moved due to random motions in the early universe, before they slowed due to cosmic expansion\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 this is an important distance called the "free streaming length" (FSL). Primordial density fluctuations smaller than this length get washed out as particles spread from overdense to underdense regions, while larger fluctuations are unaffected; therefore this length sets a minimum scale for later structure formation. The categories are set with respect to the size of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Protogalaxy" title="Protogalaxy">\n        protogalaxy\n       </a>\n       (an object that later evolves into a dwarf galaxy): dark matter particles are classified as cold, warm, or hot according as their FSL; much smaller (cold), similar (warm), or much larger (hot) than a protogalaxy.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">\n        <a href="#cite_note-88">\n         [86]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">\n        <a href="#cite_note-89">\n         [87]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Mixtures of the above are also possible: a theory of\n       <a href="/wiki/Mixed_dark_matter" title="Mixed dark matter">\n        mixed dark matter\n       </a>\n       was popular in the mid-1990s, but was rejected following the discovery of\n       <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">\n        dark energy\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Cold dark matter leads to a "bottom-up" formation of structure while hot dark matter would result in a "top-down" formation scenario; the latter is excluded by high-redshift galaxy observations.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_17-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-17">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_definitions">\n        Alternative definitions\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Alternative definitions">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       These categories also correspond to\n       <a href="/wiki/Fluctuation_spectrum" title="Fluctuation spectrum">\n        fluctuation spectrum\n       </a>\n       effects and the interval following the Big Bang at which each type became non-relativistic. Davis\n       <i>\n        et al.\n       </i>\n       wrote in 1985:\n      </p>\n      <table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">\n       <tr>\n        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">\n         \xe2\x80\x9c\n        </td>\n        <td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">\n         Candidate particles can be grouped into three categories on the basis of their effect on the\n         <a href="/wiki/Mixed_dark_matter" title="Mixed dark matter">\n          fluctuation spectrum\n         </a>\n         (Bond\n         <i>\n          et al.\n         </i>\n         1983). If the dark matter is composed of abundant light particles which remain relativistic until shortly before recombination, then it may be termed "hot". The best candidate for hot dark matter is a neutrino ... A second possibility is for the dark matter particles to interact more weakly than neutrinos, to be less abundant, and to have a mass of order 1\xc2\xa0keV. Such particles are termed "warm dark matter", because they have lower thermal velocities than massive neutrinos ... there are at present few candidate particles which fit this description.\n         <a href="/wiki/Gravitino" title="Gravitino">\n          Gravitinos\n         </a>\n         and\n         <a href="/wiki/Photino" title="Photino">\n          photinos\n         </a>\n         have been suggested (Pagels and Primack 1982; Bond, Szalay and Turner 1982) ... Any particles which became nonrelativistic very early, and so were able to diffuse a negligible distance, are termed "cold" dark matter (CDM). There are many candidates for CDM including supersymmetric particles.\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">\n          <a href="#cite_note-90">\n           [88]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </td>\n        <td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: \'Times New Roman\', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">\n         \xe2\x80\x9d\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       Another approximate dividing line is that "warm" dark matter became non-relativistic when the universe was approximately 1 year old and 1 millionth of its present size and in the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation-dominated_era" title="Radiation-dominated era">\n        radiation-dominated era\n       </a>\n       (photons and neutrinos), with a photon temperature 2.7 million K. Standard physical cosmology gives the\n       <a href="/wiki/Particle_horizon" title="Particle horizon">\n        particle horizon\n       </a>\n       size as 2\n       <i>\n        ct\n       </i>\n       (speed of light multiplied by time) in the radiation-dominated era, thus 2 light-years. A region of this size would expand to 2 million light years today (absent structure formation). The actual FSL is roughly 5 times the above length, since it continues to grow slowly as particle velocities decrease inversely with the scale factor after they become non-relativistic. In this example the FSL would correspond to 10 million light-years or 3 Mpc today, around the size containing an average large galaxy.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The 2.7 million K photon temperature gives a typical photon energy of 250 electron-volts, thereby setting a typical mass scale for "warm" dark matter: particles much more massive than this, such as GeV\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x93 TeV mass\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WIMPs" title="WIMPs">\n        WIMPs\n       </a>\n       , would become non-relativistic much earlier than 1 year after the Big Bang and thus have FSLs much smaller than a protogalaxy, making them "cold". Conversely, much lighter particles, such as neutrinos with masses of only a few eV, have FSLs much larger than a protogalaxy, thus qualifying them as "hot".\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_dark_matter">\n        Cold dark matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Cold dark matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Cold_dark_matter" title="Cold dark matter">\n        Cold dark matter\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       "Cold" dark matter offers the simplest explanation for most cosmological observations. It is dark matter composed of constituents with an FSL much smaller than a protogalaxy. This is the focus for dark matter research, as hot dark matter does not seem to be capable of supporting galaxy or galaxy cluster formation, and most particle candidates slowed early.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The constituents of "cold" dark matter are unknown. Possibilities range from large objects like MACHOs (such as black holes\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawkins_91-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Hawkins-91">\n         [89]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       ) or\n       <a href="/wiki/Robust_associations_of_massive_baryonic_objects" title="Robust associations of massive baryonic objects">\n        RAMBOs\n       </a>\n       (such as clusters of brown dwarfs), to new particles such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WIMPs" title="WIMPs">\n        WIMPs\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axions" title="Axions">\n        axions\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Studies of\n       <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">\n        Big Bang nucleosynthesis\n       </a>\n       and gravitational lensing convinced most cosmologists\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_17-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-17">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_92-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Carr-92">\n         [90]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_93-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Peter-93">\n         [91]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrett_94-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Garrett-94">\n         [92]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertone_95-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bertone-95">\n         [93]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olive_96-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Olive-96">\n         [94]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       that MACHOs\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_92-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Carr-92">\n         [90]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garrett_94-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Garrett-94">\n         [92]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       cannot make up more than a small fraction of dark matter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_1995_15-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Copi_1995-15">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carr_92-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Carr-92">\n         [90]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       According to A. Peter: "... the only\n       <i>\n        really plausible\n       </i>\n       dark-matter candidates are new particles."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peter_93-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Peter-93">\n         [91]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The 1997\n       <a href="/wiki/DAMA/NaI" title="DAMA/NaI">\n        DAMA/NaI\n       </a>\n       experiment and its successor\n       <a href="/wiki/DAMA/LIBRA" title="DAMA/LIBRA">\n        DAMA/LIBRA\n       </a>\n       in 2013, claimed to directly detect dark matter particles passing through the Earth, but many researchers remain skeptical, as negative results from similar experiments seem incompatible with the DAMA results.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many\n       <a href="/wiki/Supersymmetry" title="Supersymmetry">\n        supersymmetric\n       </a>\n       models offer dark matter candidates in the form of the WIMPy\n       <a href="/wiki/Lightest_Supersymmetric_Particle" title="Lightest Supersymmetric Particle">\n        Lightest Supersymmetric Particle\n       </a>\n       (LSP).\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">\n        <a href="#cite_note-97">\n         [95]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Separately, heavy sterile neutrinos exist in non-supersymmetric extensions to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">\n        standard model\n       </a>\n       that explain the small\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrino_mass" title="Neutrino mass">\n        neutrino mass\n       </a>\n       through the\n       <a href="/wiki/Seesaw_mechanism" title="Seesaw mechanism">\n        seesaw mechanism\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Warm_dark_matter">\n        Warm dark matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Warm dark matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Warm_dark_matter" title="Warm dark matter">\n        Warm dark matter\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       "Warm" dark matter refers to particles with an FSL comparable to the size of a protogalaxy. Predictions based on warm dark matter are similar to those for cold dark matter on large scales, but with less small-scale density perturbations. This reduces the predicted abundance of dwarf galaxies and may lead to lower density of dark matter in the central parts of large galaxies; some researchers consider this to be a better fit to observations. A challenge for this model is the lack of particle candidates with the required mass ~ 300 eV to 3000 eV.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       No known particles can be categorized as "warm" dark matter. A postulated candidate is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Sterile_neutrino" title="Sterile neutrino">\n        sterile neutrino\n       </a>\n       : a heavier, slower form of neutrino that does not interact through the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_force" title="Weak force">\n        weak force\n       </a>\n       , unlike other neutrinos. Some modified gravity theories, such as\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scalar-tensor-vector_gravity" title="Scalar-tensor-vector gravity">\n        scalar-tensor-vector gravity\n       </a>\n       , require "warm" dark matter to make their equations work.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Hot_dark_matter">\n        Hot dark matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Hot dark matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Hot_dark_matter" title="Hot dark matter">\n        Hot dark matter\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       "Hot" dark matter consists of particles whose FSL is much larger than the size of a protogalaxy. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">\n        neutrino\n       </a>\n       qualifies as such particle. They were discovered independently, long before the hunt for dark matter: they were postulated in 1930, and\n       <a href="/wiki/Cowan%E2%80%93Reines_neutrino_experiment" title="Cowan\xe2\x80\x93Reines neutrino experiment">\n        detected in 1956\n       </a>\n       . Neutrinos\'\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutrino_mass" title="Neutrino mass">\n        mass\n       </a>\n       is less than 10\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x926\n       </sup>\n       that of an\n       <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">\n        electron\n       </a>\n       . Neutrinos interact with normal matter only via gravity and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Weak_interaction" title="Weak interaction">\n        weak force\n       </a>\n       , making them difficult to detect (the weak force only works over a small distance, thus a neutrino triggers a weak force event only if it hits a nucleus head-on). This makes them \'weakly interacting light particles\' (WILPs), as opposed to WIMPs.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The three known\n       <a href="/wiki/Flavour_(particle_physics)" title="Flavour (particle physics)">\n        flavours\n       </a>\n       of neutrinos are the\n       <i>\n        electron\n       </i>\n       ,\n       <i>\n        muon\n       </i>\n       , and\n       <i>\n        tau\n       </i>\n       . Their masses are slightly different. Neutrinos oscillate among the flavours as they move. It is hard to determine an exact\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_bound" title="Upper bound">\n        upper bound\n       </a>\n       on the collective average mass of the three neutrinos (or for any of the three individually). For example, if the average neutrino mass were over 50\n       <i>\n        eV/c\n       </i>\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       (less than 10\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x925\n       </sup>\n       of the mass of an electron), the universe would collapse. CMB data and other methods indicate that their average mass probably does not exceed 0.3\n       <i>\n        eV/c\n       </i>\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       . Thus, observed neutrinos cannot explain dark matter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">\n        <a href="#cite_note-98">\n         [96]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Because galaxy-size density fluctuations get washed out by free-streaming, "hot" dark matter implies that the first objects that can form are huge supercluster-size pancakes, which then fragment into galaxies.\n       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Deep_Fields" title="List of Deep Fields">\n        Deep-field observations\n       </a>\n       show instead that galaxies formed first, followed by clusters and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Superclusters" title="Superclusters">\n        superclusters\n       </a>\n       as galaxies clump together.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Detection_of_dark_matter_particles">\n        Detection of dark matter particles\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Detection of dark matter particles">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       If dark matter is made up of sub-atomic particles, then millions, possibly billions, of such particles must pass through every square centimeter of the Earth each second.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaitskell_2004_99-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Gaitskell_2004-99">\n         [97]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Number_per_second_100-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Number_per_second-100">\n         [98]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Many experiments aim to test this hypothesis. Although\n       <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">\n        WIMPs\n       </a>\n       are popular search candidates,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_17-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-17">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       the Axion Dark Matter eXperiment (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ADMX" title="ADMX">\n        ADMX\n       </a>\n       ) searches for\n       <a href="/wiki/Axion" title="Axion">\n        axions\n       </a>\n       . Another candidate is heavy\n       <a href="/wiki/Hidden_sector" title="Hidden sector">\n        hidden sector\n       </a>\n       particles that only interact with ordinary matter via gravity.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       These experiments can be divided into two classes: direct detection experiments, which search for the scattering of dark matter particles off atomic nuclei within a detector; and indirect detection, which look for the products of dark matter particle annihilations or decays.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_86-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-86">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Direct_detection">\n        Direct detection\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Direct detection">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       For more details on this topic, see\n       <a href="/wiki/Weakly_interacting_massive_particles#Direct_detection" title="Weakly interacting massive particles">\n        Weakly interacting massive particles \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa0Direct detection\n       </a>\n       .\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Direct detection experiments aim to observe low-energy recoils (typically a few\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KeV" title="KeV">\n        keVs\n       </a>\n       ) of nuclei induced by interactions with particles of dark matter, which (in theory) are passing through the Earth. After such a recoil the nucleus will emit energy as e.g.\n       <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">\n        scintillation\n       </a>\n       light or\n       <a href="/wiki/Phonon" title="Phonon">\n        phonons\n       </a>\n       , which is then detected by sensitive apparatus. In order to do this effectively it is crucial to maintain a low background, and so such experiments operate deep underground to reduce the interference from\n       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">\n        cosmic rays\n       </a>\n       . Examples of underground laboratories which house direct detection experiments include the\n       <a href="/wiki/Stawell_Underground_Physics_Laboratory" title="Stawell Underground Physics Laboratory">\n        Stawell mine\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soudan_mine" title="Soudan mine">\n        Soudan mine\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/SNOLAB" title="SNOLAB">\n        SNOLAB\n       </a>\n       underground laboratory at\n       <a href="/wiki/Greater_Sudbury" title="Greater Sudbury">\n        Sudbury\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gran_Sasso_National_Laboratory" title="Gran Sasso National Laboratory">\n        Gran Sasso National Laboratory\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/Canfranc_Underground_Laboratory" title="Canfranc Underground Laboratory">\n        Canfranc Underground Laboratory\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boulby_Underground_Laboratory" title="Boulby Underground Laboratory">\n        Boulby Underground Laboratory\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Underground_Science_and_Engineering_Laboratory" title="Deep Underground Science and Engineering Laboratory">\n        Deep Underground Science and Engineering Laboratory\n       </a>\n       and the\n       <a href="/wiki/China_Jinping_Underground_Laboratory" title="China Jinping Underground Laboratory">\n        China Jinping Underground Laboratory\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       These experiments mostly use either cryogenic or noble liquid detector technologies. Cryogenic detectors operating at temperatures below 100mK, detect the heat produced when a particle hits an atom in a crystal absorber such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">\n        germanium\n       </a>\n       .\n       <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">\n        Noble liquid\n       </a>\n       detectors detect\n       <a href="/wiki/Scintillation_(physics)" title="Scintillation (physics)">\n        scintillation\n       </a>\n       produced by a particle collision in liquid\n       <a href="/wiki/Xenon" title="Xenon">\n        xenon\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Argon" title="Argon">\n        argon\n       </a>\n       . Cryogenic detector experiments include:\n       <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_Dark_Matter_Search" title="Cryogenic Dark Matter Search">\n        CDMS\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/Cryogenic_Rare_Event_Search_with_Superconducting_Thermometers" title="Cryogenic Rare Event Search with Superconducting Thermometers">\n        CRESST\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/EDELWEISS" title="EDELWEISS">\n        EDELWEISS\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/European_Underground_Rare_Event_Calorimeter_Array" title="European Underground Rare Event Calorimeter Array">\n        EURECA\n       </a>\n       . Noble liquid experiments include ZEPLIN,\n       <a href="/wiki/XENON" title="XENON">\n        XENON\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/DEAP" title="DEAP">\n        DEAP\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/ArDM" title="ArDM">\n        ArDM\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/WIMP_Argon_Programme" title="WIMP Argon Programme">\n        WARP\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/DarkSide" title="DarkSide">\n        DarkSide\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a href="/wiki/PandaX" title="PandaX">\n        PandaX\n       </a>\n       , and LUX, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Large_Underground_Xenon_experiment" title="Large Underground Xenon experiment">\n        Large Underground Xenon experiment\n       </a>\n       . Both of these techniques focus strongly on their ability to distinguish background particles (which predominantly scatter off electrons) from dark matter particles (that scatter off nuclei). Other experiments include\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SIMPLE_(dark_matter)" title="SIMPLE (dark matter)">\n        SIMPLE\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PICASSO_(dark_matter)" title="PICASSO (dark matter)">\n        PICASSO\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Currently there has been no well-established claim of dark matter detection from a direct detection experiment, leading instead to strong upper limits on the mass and interaction cross section with nucleons of such dark matter particles.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">\n        <a href="#cite_note-101">\n         [99]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/DAMA/NaI" title="DAMA/NaI">\n        DAMA/NaI\n       </a>\n       and more recent\n       <a href="/wiki/DAMA/LIBRA" title="DAMA/LIBRA">\n        DAMA/LIBRA\n       </a>\n       experimental collaborations claim to have detected an annual modulation in the rate of events in their detectors,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">\n        <a href="#cite_note-102">\n         [100]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">\n        <a href="#cite_note-103">\n         [101]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       which they claim is due to dark matter. This results from the expectation that as the Earth orbits the Sun, the velocity of the detector relative to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_halo" title="Dark matter halo">\n        dark matter halo\n       </a>\n       will vary by a small amount. This claim is so far unconfirmed and in contradiction with negative results from other experiments such as LUX and SuperCDMS.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">\n        <a href="#cite_note-104">\n         [102]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A special case of direct detection experiments covers those with directional sensitivity. This is a search strategy based on the motion of the Solar System around the\n       <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">\n        Galactic Center\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apssyn_105-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-apssyn-105">\n         [103]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-samlee_106-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-samlee-106">\n         [104]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dmgsheff_107-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-dmgsheff-107">\n         [105]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kavli_108-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Kavli-108">\n         [106]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A low pressure\n       <a href="/wiki/Time_projection_chamber" title="Time projection chamber">\n        time projection chamber\n       </a>\n       makes it possible to access information on recoiling tracks and constrain WIMP-nucleus kinematics. WIMPs coming from the direction in which the Sun is travelling (roughly towards\n       <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(constellation)" title="Cygnus (constellation)">\n        Cygnus\n       </a>\n       ) may then be separated from background, which should be isotropic. Directional dark matter experiments include\n       <a href="/wiki/Dark_Matter_Time_Projection_Chamber" title="Dark Matter Time Projection Chamber">\n        DMTPC\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Directional_Recoil_Identification_From_Tracks" title="Directional Recoil Identification From Tracks">\n        DRIFT\n       </a>\n       , Newage and MIMAC.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indirect_detection">\n        Indirect detection\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Indirect detection">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1280" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/220px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/330px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg/440px-Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Collage_of_six_cluster_collisions_with_dark_matter_maps.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Collage of six cluster collisions with dark matter maps. The clusters were observed in a study of how dark matter in clusters of galaxies behaves when the clusters collide.\n         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">\n          <a href="#cite_note-109">\n           [107]\n          </a>\n         </sup>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_6" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:854px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_7" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/854px--Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:854px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="193.323" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.480p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="HD WebM (720P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 720P" data-transcodekey="720p.webm" data-width="1280" data-height="720" data-bandwidth="1964864" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.1080p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Full HD WebM (1080P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 1080P" data-transcodekey="1080p.webm" data-width="1920" data-height="1080" data-bandwidth="2203552" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.160p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth WebM (160P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 160P" data-transcodekey="160p.webm" data-width="284" data-height="160" data-bandwidth="220296" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.240p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" data-bandwidth="322456" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;" data-title="WebM (360P)" data-shorttitle="WebM 360P" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" data-bandwidth="572488" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'>\n         <img alt="File:Turning Black Holes into Dark Matter Labs.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm/220px--Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>\n         <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d0/Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" target="new" title="Play media">\n          <span class="play-btn-large">\n           <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">\n            Play media\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Turning_Black_Holes_into_Dark_Matter_Labs.webm" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Video about the potential\n         <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_astronomy" title="Gamma-ray astronomy">\n          gamma-ray detection\n         </a>\n         of dark matter\n         <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">\n          annihilation\n         </a>\n         around\n         <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">\n          supermassive black holes\n         </a>\n         .\n         <i>\n          (Duration 3:13, also see file description.)\n         </i>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Indirect detection experiments search for the products of the self-annihilation or decay of dark matter particles in outer space. For example, in regions of high dark matter density (e.g. the\n       <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Center" title="Galactic Center">\n        centre of our galaxy\n       </a>\n       ) two dark matter particles could\n       <a href="/wiki/Annihilation" title="Annihilation">\n        annihilate\n       </a>\n       to produce\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gamma_rays" title="Gamma rays">\n        gamma rays\n       </a>\n       or Standard Model particle-antiparticle pairs.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bertone2010_110-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Bertone2010-110">\n         [108]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Alternatively if the dark matter particle is unstable, it could decay into standard model (or other) particles. These processes could be detected indirectly through an excess of gamma rays,\n       <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">\n        antiprotons\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">\n        positrons\n       </a>\n       emanating from high density regions in our galaxy or others.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">\n        <a href="#cite_note-111">\n         [109]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       A major difficulty inherent in such searches is that there are various astrophysical sources which can mimic the signal expected from dark matter, and so multiple signals will likely be required for a conclusive discovery.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_17-5">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-17">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_merritt_86-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_merritt-86">\n         [84]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A few of the dark matter particles passing through the Sun or Earth may scatter off atoms and lose energy. Thus dark matter may accumulate at the center of these bodies, increasing the chance of collision/annihilation. This could produce a distinctive signal in the form of high-energy\n       <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">\n        neutrinos\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">\n        <a href="#cite_note-112">\n         [110]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Such a signal would be strong indirect proof of WIMP dark matter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bertone_hooper_silk_17-6">\n        <a href="#cite_note-bertone_hooper_silk-17">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       High-energy neutrino telescopes such as\n       <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Muon_And_Neutrino_Detector_Array" title="Antarctic Muon And Neutrino Detector Array">\n        AMANDA\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IceCube" title="IceCube">\n        IceCube\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/ANTARES_(telescope)" title="ANTARES (telescope)">\n        ANTARES\n       </a>\n       are searching for this signal.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERandall2015298_113-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall2015298-113">\n         [111]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The detection by\n       <a href="/wiki/LIGO" title="LIGO">\n        LIGO\n       </a>\n       in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GW150914" title="GW150914">\n        September 2015\n       </a>\n       of gravitational waves, opens the possibility of observing dark matter in a new way, particularly if it is the form of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_black_holes" title="Primordial black holes">\n        primordial black holes\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">\n        <a href="#cite_note-114">\n         [112]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">\n        <a href="#cite_note-115">\n         [113]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">\n        <a href="#cite_note-116">\n         [114]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Many experimental searches have been undertaken to look for such emission from dark matter annihilation or decay, examples of which follow. The\n       <a href="/wiki/Energetic_Gamma_Ray_Experiment_Telescope" title="Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope">\n        Energetic Gamma Ray Experiment Telescope\n       </a>\n       observed more gamma rays in 2008 than expected from the Milky Way, but scientists concluded that this was most likely due to incorrect estimation of the telescope\'s sensitivity.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">\n        <a href="#cite_note-117">\n         [115]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">\n        Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope\n       </a>\n       is searching for similar gamma rays.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">\n        <a href="#cite_note-118">\n         [116]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In April 2012, an analysis of previously available data from its\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_LAT" title="Fermi LAT">\n        Large Area Telescope\n       </a>\n       instrument produced statistical evidence of a 130 GeV signal in the gamma radiation coming from the center of the Milky Way.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">\n        <a href="#cite_note-119">\n         [117]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       WIMP annihilation was seen as the most probable explanation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">\n        <a href="#cite_note-120">\n         [118]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       At higher energies,\n       <a href="/wiki/IACT" title="IACT">\n        ground-based gamma-ray telescopes\n       </a>\n       have set limits on the annihilation of dark matter in dwarf spheroidal galaxies\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">\n        <a href="#cite_note-121">\n         [119]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and in clusters of galaxies.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">\n        <a href="#cite_note-122">\n         [120]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Payload_for_Antimatter_Matter_Exploration_and_Light-nuclei_Astrophysics" title="Payload for Antimatter Matter Exploration and Light-nuclei Astrophysics">\n        PAMELA\n       </a>\n       experiment (launched 2006) detected excess\n       <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">\n        positrons\n       </a>\n       . They could be from dark matter annihilation or from\n       <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">\n        pulsars\n       </a>\n       . No excess\n       <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">\n        antiprotons\n       </a>\n       were observed.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">\n        <a href="#cite_note-123">\n         [121]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In 2013 results from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">\n        Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer\n       </a>\n       on the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">\n        International Space Station\n       </a>\n       indicated excess high-energy\n       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">\n        cosmic rays\n       </a>\n       that could be due to dark matter annihilation.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APS-20130403_124-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-APS-20130403-124">\n         [122]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMS-20130403_125-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-AMS-20130403-125">\n         [123]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AP-20130403_126-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-AP-20130403-126">\n         [124]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20130403_127-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-BBC-20130403-127">\n         [125]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20130403_128-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130403-128">\n         [126]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130403_129-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130403-129">\n         [127]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Collider_searches_for_dark_matter">\n        Collider searches for dark matter\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Collider searches for dark matter">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       An alternative approach to the detection of dark matter particles in nature is to produce them in a laboratory. Experiments with the\n       <a href="/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider" title="Large Hadron Collider">\n        Large Hadron Collider\n       </a>\n       (LHC) may be able to detect dark matter particles produced in collisions of the LHC\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        proton\n       </a>\n       beams. Because a dark matter particle should have negligible interactions with normal visible matter, it may be detected indirectly as (large amounts of) missing energy and momentum that escape the detectors, provided other (non-negligible) collision products are detected.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kane_watson_130-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-kane_watson-130">\n         [128]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Constraints on dark matter also exist from the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LEP" title="LEP">\n        LEP\n       </a>\n       experiment using a similar principle, but probing the interaction of dark matter particles with electrons rather than quarks.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">\n        <a href="#cite_note-131">\n         [129]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It is important to note that any discovery from collider searches will need to be corroborated by discoveries in the indirect or direct detection sectors, in order to prove that the particle discovered is in fact the dark matter of our Universe.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_theories">\n        Alternative theories\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Alternative theories">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       For more details on this topic, see\n       <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">\n        Alternatives to general relativity\n       </a>\n       .\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Because dark matter remains to be conclusively identified, many theories that aim to explain the observational evidence without invoking dark matter have emerged. The obvious way to do this is to modify general relativity. General relativity is well-tested on solar-system scales, but its validity on galactic or cosmological scales is less certain. A suitable modification to general relativity can conceivably eliminate the need for dark matter. The most well-known theories of this class are\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modified_Newtonian_Dynamics" title="Modified Newtonian Dynamics">\n        MOND\n       </a>\n       and its relativistic generalization\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TeVeS" title="TeVeS">\n        TeVeS\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">\n        <a href="#cite_note-132">\n         [130]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/F(R)_gravity" title="F(R) gravity">\n        f(R) gravity\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">\n        <a href="#cite_note-133">\n         [131]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity">\n        entropic gravity\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physorgnewtheory_134-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-physorgnewtheory-134">\n         [132]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Alternatives_to_general_relativity" title="Alternatives to general relativity">\n        Alternative theories\n       </a>\n       abound.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">\n        <a href="#cite_note-135">\n         [133]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">\n        <a href="#cite_note-136">\n         [134]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A problem with alternative theories is that the observational evidence for dark matter comes from so many independent angles (see the "observational evidence" section above). Any alternative theory not only has to explain all the evidence, it also has to explain individual cases such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Bullet_Cluster" title="Bullet Cluster">\n        Bullet Cluster\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">\n        <a href="#cite_note-137">\n         [135]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       wherein observations indicate that the location of the center of mass is far away from the center of baryonic mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">\n        <a href="#cite_note-138">\n         [136]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Nonetheless, there has been some scattered successes for alternative theories, such as a 2016 test of gravitational lensing in entropic gravity.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">\n        <a href="#cite_note-139">\n         [137]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">\n        <a href="#cite_note-140">\n         [138]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">\n        <a href="#cite_note-141">\n         [139]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The prevailing opinion among most astrophysicists is that while modifications to general relativity can conceivably explain part of the observational evidence, there is probably enough data to conclude there must still be some dark matter.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">\n        <a href="#cite_note-142">\n         [140]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_philosophy_of_science">\n        In philosophy of science\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: In philosophy of science">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_science" title="Philosophy of science">\n        philosophy of science\n       </a>\n       , dark matter is an example of an "auxiliary hypothesis", an\n       <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypothesis" title="Ad hoc hypothesis">\n        ad hoc\n       </a>\n       postulate that is added to a theory in response to observations that\n       <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">\n        falsify\n       </a>\n       it. It has been argued that the dark matter hypothesis is a\n       <a href="/wiki/Conventionalism#Epistemology" title="Conventionalism">\n        conventionalist\n       </a>\n       hypothesis, that is, a hypothesis that adds no empirical content and hence is unfalsifiable in the sense defined by\n       <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">\n        Karl Popper\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">\n        <a href="#cite_note-143">\n         [141]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">\n        In popular culture\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: In popular culture">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter_in_fiction" title="Dark matter in fiction">\n        Dark matter in fiction\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Mention of dark matter is made in works of fiction. In such cases, it is usually attributed extraordinary physical or magical properties. Such descriptions are often inconsistent with the hypothesized properties of dark matter in physics and cosmology.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">\n        See also\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: See also">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="columns-2" style="background-color: transparent; width: 100%">\n       <div class="column">\n       </div>\n       <div class="column">\n        <dl>\n         <dt>\n          Related theories\n         </dt>\n        </dl>\n        <ul>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">\n           Dark energy\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Conformal_gravity" title="Conformal gravity">\n           Conformal gravity\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Entropic_gravity" title="Entropic gravity">\n           Entropic gravity\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dark_electromagnetism" title="Dark electromagnetism">\n           Dark electromagnetism\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Massive_gravity" title="Massive gravity">\n           Massive gravity\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Unparticle_physics" title="Unparticle physics">\n           Unparticle physics\n          </a>\n         </li>\n        </ul>\n        <dl>\n         <dt>\n          Experiments\n         </dt>\n        </dl>\n        <ul>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/DEAP" title="DEAP">\n           DEAP\n          </a>\n          , a search apparatus\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a href="/wiki/Dark_Matter_Particle_Explorer" title="Dark Matter Particle Explorer">\n           DAMPE\n          </a>\n          , a space mission\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_Antiparticle_Spectrometer" title="General Antiparticle Spectrometer">\n           General Antiparticle Spectrometer\n          </a>\n         </li>\n         <li>\n          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multidark" title="Multidark">\n           Multidark\n          </a>\n          , particle accelerator research infrastructure\n         </li>\n        </ul>\n       </div>\n       <div class="column">\n        <dl>\n         <dt>\n          Dark matter candidates\n         </dt>\n </div>\n      </div>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">\n        Notes\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dark_matter&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Notes">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n       <div class="mw-references-wrap">\n        <ol class="references">\n         <li id="cite_note-9">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-9">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           Since dark energy, by convention, does not count as "matter", this is 26.8/(4.9 + 26.8)=0.845\n          </span>\n         </li>\n         <li id="cite_note-51">\n          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n           <b>\n            <a href="#cite_ref-51">\n             ^\n            </a>\n           </b>\n          </span>\n          <span class="reference-text">\n           This is a consequence of the\n           <a href="/wiki/Shell_theorem" title="Shell theorem">\n            shell theorem\n           </a>\n           and the observation that spiral galaxies are spherically symmetric to a large extent (in 2D).\n          </span>\n         </li>\n        </ol>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <h2>\n Johns Hopkins University. 15 June 2016\n           <span class="reference-accessdate">\n            . Retrieved\n            <span class="nowrap">\n             20 June\n            </span>\n            2015\n           </span>\n           .\n           <q>\n            While their existence has not been established with certainty, primordial black holes have in the past been suggested as a possible solution to the dark matter mystery. Because there\xe2\x80\x99s so little evidence of them, though, the primordial black hole-dark matter hypothesis has not gained a large following among scientists. The LIGO findings, however, raise the prospect anew, especially as the objects detected in that experiment conform to the mass predicted for dark matter. Predictions made by scientists in the past held that conditions at the birth of the universe would produce lots of these primordial black holes distributed roughly evenly in the universe, clustering in halos around galaxies. All this would make them good candidates for dark matter.\n           </q>\n          </cite>\n          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Did+Gravitational+Wave+Detector+Find+Dark+Matter%3F&amp;rft.pub=Johns+Hopkins+University&amp;rft.date=2016-06-15&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Freleases.jhu.edu%2F2016%2F06%2F15%2Fdid-gravitational-wave-detector-find-dark-matter%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADark+matter">\n           <span style="display:none;">\n           </span>\n          </span>\n         </span>\n        </li>\n        <li id="cite_note-116">\n         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">\n          <b>\n           <a href="#cite_ref-116">\n            ^\n           </a>\n          </b>\n         </span>\n         <span class="reference-text">\n          <cite class="citation journal">\n           Bird, Simeon;
